## 1. *Minds and Machines*

**(Philosophy of AI / Cognitive Foundations)**

**Goal here:** Conceptual necessity. You are arguing *what abstraction must be*, not how to engineer it.

### Ontological Commitments

1. Are "events" intended as **primitive ontological units**, or as a **theoretical posit justified by explanatory payoff**?
2. Is "memory" being claimed as **ontologically prior to representation**, or merely **explanatorily prior**?
3. Do you want to explicitly reject **state-based metaphysics**, or only show its insufficiency for abstraction?

### Conceptual Necessity

4. Which claims are meant to be **necessary truths** about abstraction (cannot be otherwise), versus contingent facts about current AI?
5. Is deterministic replay framed as **the only possible ground** of abstraction, or **one minimal sufficient ground**?
6. What exactly fails *in principle* for compression-only accounts, rather than just empirically?

### Relation to Existing Positions

7. Is this intended as a **replacement** for representationalism, or a **substrate below it**?
8. How explicitly do you want to position against:

   * Predictive processing?
   * Structural realism?
   * Mechanistic explanation?
9. Should replay be compared to **counterfactual dependence**, **narrative explanation**, or **structural equation models** — or deliberately kept orthogonal?

### Objection Handling

10. What level of commitment do you want regarding consciousness: strict agnosticism, deflationary stance, or principled exclusion?
11. Are you willing to concede that some "representations" emerge *after* abstraction, or do you want a harder line?

📌 *Key clarifier:*
**Is this paper making a metaphysical claim about what abstraction *is*, or an epistemic claim about what we must posit to explain it?**
Your answer determines tone and reviewer tolerance.

---

## 2. *Artificial Intelligence* (Elsevier)

**(Formal AI Theory)**

**Goal here:** Operational distinctness. You must make reviewers say: *"This is not just philosophy dressed up."*

### Formal Commitments

1. What is the **minimal formal structure** required for an "event" (tuple, transition, labeled operation)?
2. Is deterministic replay assumed as an axiom, or derived from constraints?
3. Are equivalence classes **defined extensionally** (same outputs) or **intensionally** (same replay structure)?

### Falsifiability & Payoff

4. What concrete failure mode exists that:

   * Compression succeeds at, but
   * Replay-based abstraction succeeds at *differently*?
5. Can a system pass standard benchmarks *without* abstraction under your definition?
6. What would count as a **counterexample** to your theory?

### Distinction Pressure

7. Where exactly does this depart from:

   * World models?
   * Causal graphs?
   * Program induction?
8. Is "representative collapse" algorithmic, architectural, or purely semantic?

### Scope Control

9. Are you claiming this is:

   * A necessary condition for general intelligence?
   * A sufficient condition for abstraction?
   * A diagnostic lens for existing systems?
10. How strong should the critique of current LLMs be: architectural impossibility vs missing component?

### Practical Constraints

11. Do you want to include even *toy* constructions, or remain purely formal?
12. Is computational cost a theoretical objection or an engineering footnote?

📌 *Key clarifier:*
**What does an AI researcher gain that they cannot get from robustness, generalization, or causal modeling frameworks alone?**

If that sentence isn't sharp, reviewers will stall.

---

## 3. *Cognitive Science*

**(Interdisciplinary Cognition)**

**Goal here:** Cognitive plausibility and cross-level coherence.

### Psychological Commitments

1. Is replay meant to correspond to **actual neural replay**, or is it a **functional abstraction**?
2. Does determinism matter cognitively, or is "low-noise replay" sufficient?
3. Are semantic equivalence classes assumed to be **learned**, **discovered**, or **constructed**?

### Development & Animals

4. What is the **earliest cognitive stage** where abstraction (by your definition) plausibly appears?
5. Do animals exhibit abstraction *without* language under this framework?
6. Is language an amplifier, a compression layer, or an external memory scaffold?

### Cognitive Distinctions

7. How does your abstraction differ from:

   * Categorization?
   * Schema formation?
   * Concept learning?
8. Is "semantic depth" empirically measurable, or intentionally theoretical?

### AI-Human Bridge

9. Are current AI failures framed as:

   * Missing replay?
   * Insufficient memory depth?
   * Lack of semantic stability?
10. Do you want to argue that *some* non-human animals outperform LLMs in abstraction, or keep that implicit?

### Empirical Traction

11. What experimental paradigms could, in principle, support or falsify this account?
12. Should neuroscience be cited as *supportive analogy* or *partial evidence*?

📌 *Key clarifier:*
**Is this paper explaining cognition as it exists, or redefining abstraction in a way cognition must satisfy?**

That distinction controls how bold you can be.

4. Information / Entropy / Complexity
(Entropy, Complex Systems, Information Theory audiences)
Unifying question:
How can semantic structure arise and persist in entropy-driven systems without collapsing into mere compression?
Working title (indicative)
Semantic Stability as Entropy-Constrained Replay
Outline
1. Introduction: Intelligence Beyond Compression
This section motivates the insufficiency of classical information-theoretic compression as an account of intelligence or meaning. It frames the problem: entropy reduction alone cannot explain why certain structures persist, generalize, and support counterfactual reasoning. The core thesis is introduced: semantic organization arises from
entropy-constrained replay
, not from compression alone.
2. Events, Irreversibility, and Semantic Cost
Here the paper introduces events as irreversible commitments that carry thermodynamic and informational cost. Memory is framed as an entropy-bearing structure rather than a passive store. This establishes the physical asymmetry between construction and observation.
3. Replay as Information Preservation
This section develops deterministic replay as a mechanism for preserving semantic information across time. Replay is contrasted with lossy compression: while compression reduces description length, replay preserves causal and semantic structure under reconstruction.
4. Semantic Equivalence and Entropy Reduction
Abstraction is introduced as the stabilization of equivalence classes over replayable histories. The key distinction is made explicit: compression reduces entropy by discarding detail, whereas abstraction reduces entropy by
identifying invariants
. This section shows how equivalence collapse can reduce entropy without information loss at the semantic level.
5. Stability, Perturbation, and Semantic Basins
This section formalizes stability as resistance to perturbations in construction history. Semantic basins are introduced as regions of low effective entropy where meaning remains invariant under replay variations. Analogies to attractors and dissipative structures are developed carefully.
6. Phase Transitions in Semantic Organization
The paper argues that abstraction emerges via phase transitions rather than gradual compression. Below a threshold, semantic equivalence is unstable; above it, abstraction locks in. This is framed using concepts from statistical mechanics and complexity theory.
7. Relation to Biological and Artificial Systems
Without mentioning specific AI systems, this section explains why purely statistical learners plateau: they compress signals but do not generate stable semantic basins. Biological systems are framed as entropy-exporting structures that maintain semantic invariants.
8. Discussion: Entropy, Meaning, and Irreversibility
This section synthesizes the argument, emphasizing that meaning is not free: it requires entropy expenditure and structural stabilization. Intelligence is reframed as the ability to
pay entropy costs to preserve semantic invariants
.
9. Conclusion
The paper concludes by reframing intelligence as an entropy-regulated phenomenon, where abstraction is the maintenance of low-entropy semantic structure under replay.
Appendices (Entropy)
Appendix A: Thermodynamic Interpretation of Events
Formal discussion of entropy production per event.
Appendix B: Stability Measures and Perturbation Bounds
Sketches of how semantic stability could be quantified using entropy or diffusion metrics.
5. Systems / OS / PL Theory
(Operating systems, programming languages, distributed systems)
Unifying question:
What would an operating system look like if semantic consistency, not resource scheduling, were its primary invariant?
Working title
A Semantic Operating System Based on Deterministic Replay
Outline
1. Introduction: The Limits of State-Based Systems
This section critiques traditional OS and PL abstractions: mutable state, hidden side effects, and weak replayability. The motivation is framed in terms of collaboration, debugging, and semantic drift.
2. Event Logs as Semantic Substrate
Events are introduced as the sole authoritative substrate. This section positions Spherepop OS within the lineage of event sourcing and log-structured systems, while emphasizing its stronger semantic commitments.
3. Deterministic Replay as Kernel Primitive
Replay is formalized as a kernel-level invariant rather than a debugging tool. The argument is that determinism is necessary for semantic inspection, counterfactual reasoning, and collaboration.
4. Semantic Objects, Relations, and Equivalence
This section introduces semantic objects and relations as first-class kernel entities. Equivalence collapse is presented as a semantic operation, not a garbage-collection or optimization step.
5. Abstraction via Representative Collapse
Abstraction is framed as reducing the number of semantic representatives required to explain observed behavior. This is contrasted with memoization, caching, and deduplication.
6. Views, Diffs, and Non-Interference
The strict separation between authoritative state and derived views is developed. Layout, visualization, and speculation are treated as non-causal overlays, ensuring kernel purity.
7. Speculation and Counterfactual Execution
This section introduces speculative branches as first-class but non-authoritative. It connects replay-based speculation to debugging, planning, and hypothetical execution in PL theory.
8. Consistency, Collaboration, and Single Sequencer Model
The arbiter model is presented as a semantic consistency mechanism rather than a performance bottleneck. The argument emphasizes total causal order as a semantic, not merely distributed-systems, requirement.
9. Comparison to Existing Systems
Spherepop OS is compared to CRDTs, databases, version control systems, and traditional kernels, highlighting where semantic invariants are stronger.
10. Conclusion: Operating Systems for Semantic Time
The paper concludes by proposing a shift in OS design: from managing resources to managing semantic history.
Appendices (Systems)
Appendix A: Operational Semantics of Kernel Events
Formal transition rules.
Appendix B: Implementation Notes
Practical considerations without weakening semantic guarantees.
6. Physics / Structural Mathematics
(Foundations, geometry, invariants, mathematically inclined audiences)
Unifying question:
What mathematical structures are required for meaning to be invariant under transformation?
Working title
Semantic Invariance and Replay in Structured State Spaces
Outline
1. Introduction: Meaning as Invariance
This section frames meaning as an invariant under transformation, drawing parallels to symmetry principles in physics and mathematics. The problem: most computational models lack a notion of semantic invariance.
2. Events as Generators of Structure
Events are introduced as generators in a structured space. Rather than states, the focus is on morphisms and constructions. Time enters as an ordering constraint, not a dimension.
3. Replay as a Structural Constraint
Replay is formalized as a requirement that constructions be reconstructible. This section draws analogies to flat connections, holonomy, and path-independence without overcommitting to any single formalism.
4. Equivalence Classes and Quotient Spaces
Abstraction is framed as passing to a quotient space under an equivalence relation induced by replay invariance. Semantic collapse is treated as identifying orbits under transformation groups.
5. Geometry of Semantic Spaces
This section develops an informal geometry of semantic space, where distance corresponds to divergence under replay and curvature corresponds to sensitivity to perturbation.
6. Stability as Topological Invariance
Stability is reframed as topological robustness: small perturbations do not change equivalence class. Connections to homotopy, stratification, and fiber bundles are discussed at a high level.
7. Phase Transitions and Structural Change
The emergence of abstraction is framed as a change in the topology of semantic space. This section discusses bifurcations, symmetry breaking, and regime shifts.
8. Relation to Physical and Computational Systems
The paper argues that semantic invariance appears in physical theories, biological systems, and computation when replay-like constraints exist.
9. Discussion: Structure Before Interpretation
This section emphasizes that semantics arises from structural constraints, not interpretation layered atop structure.
10. Conclusion
Meaning is reframed as a structural invariant. Replay is identified as the minimal condition for semantic geometry.
Appendices (Structural Math)
Appendix A: Formal Correspondence Sketches
Connections to category theory, topology, and geometry (intentionally non-committal).

Excellent — these are already *well-gauged*. Below are **journal-facing clarifying questions** for **each of the three remaining versions**, tuned to the kinds of objections, misunderstandings, and scope-creep risks that reviewers in those communities reliably bring.

As before, these are **not requests for new content**; they are questions that determine *how hard*, *how formal*, and *how ontologically committed* each paper should be.

# 4. Information / Entropy / Complexity

**(Entropy, complex systems, information theory)**

**Primary risk:**
Reviewers collapsing your argument back into "just another compression/information-theoretic metaphor" or demanding unnecessary thermodynamic rigor.

### Conceptual Commitments

1. Is entropy here **physical thermodynamic entropy**, **Shannon entropy**, or an **effective/semantic entropy** — and where are you explicitly *not* identifying them?
2. Are "events" assumed to have a literal thermodynamic cost, or is that cost **conceptual but constrained by physics**?
3. Is semantic information conserved under replay *in principle*, or only under idealized constraints?

### Compression vs Abstraction Boundary

4. What is the *strongest possible* statement you want to make about why compression cannot yield semantic invariance?
5. Are there forms of compression that *approximate* abstraction, or is the distinction categorical?
6. Is representative collapse allowed to increase micro-entropy while reducing macro-entropy?

### Stability & Phase Transitions

7. Is the phase transition claim meant:

   * metaphorically,
   * structurally (complex systems sense),
   * or mathematically (order parameter, threshold behavior)?
8. What exactly is the control parameter for abstraction emergence: memory depth, replay fidelity, entropy export rate?
9. Can semantic basins overlap, merge, or split — and does that matter for the thesis?

### Relation to Existing Frameworks

10. How explicitly do you want to contrast against:

    * Minimum description length?
    * Information bottleneck?
    * Free energy principle?
11. Do you want reviewers to read this as **compatible with**, or **corrective to**, mainstream information theory?

### Measurement & Formal Pressure

12. Are the appendix sketches meant to suggest *possible future metrics*, or to imply near-term quantification?
13. What would count as a **failure case** for entropy-constrained replay?

📌 **Key clarifier:**
**Is entropy here a constraint on semantics, or the explanatory basis of semantics itself?**
Pick one — ambiguity here will invite reviewer overreach.

---

# 5. Systems / OS / PL Theory

**(Operating systems, PL, distributed systems)**

**Primary risk:**
Reviewers treating this as either (a) an over-engineered event-sourcing system, or (b) an under-specified philosophy of systems.

### Scope & Ambition

1. Is Spherepop OS intended as:

   * a conceptual OS model,
   * a research OS,
   * or a set of semantic invariants applicable to many systems?
2. Are you claiming semantic consistency as **strictly stronger** than existing consistency models, or merely orthogonal?

### Determinism & Replay

3. How absolute is determinism: bitwise, logical, or semantic?
4. What forms of nondeterminism are allowed (IO timing, concurrency scheduling), and how are they semantically quarantined?
5. Is replay a *design-time* invariant, or a *runtime-enforced* property?

### Event Authority

6. Are events purely append-only, or can they be semantically "revoked" via higher-order events?
7. Is the single sequencer model a **logical requirement** or an **implementation convenience**?
8. How do you want to position against CRDT advocates who reject total order?

### Abstraction Mechanics

9. Is representative collapse:

   * automatic,
   * user-driven,
   * or semantically inferred?
10. What prevents premature or incorrect abstraction from poisoning replay?
11. Can abstractions be revised, or are they irreversible commitments?

### PL & OS Boundary

12. Is this OS enforcing semantics *below* the programming language, or exposing primitives *to* the language?
13. Do types, effects, or contracts play any formal role, or are they intentionally external?

### Evaluation Pressure

14. Do you want reviewers to imagine benchmarks, or explicitly *not*?
15. What would a minimal "toy Spherepop kernel" have to demonstrate to count as success?

📌 **Key clarifier:**
**Is semantic consistency an invariant stronger than correctness, or a different axis altogether?**
Your answer determines whether PL reviewers treat this as radical or naïve.

---

# 6. Physics / Structural Mathematics

**(Foundations, geometry, invariants)**

**Primary risk:**
Being pushed either into over-formalization (category theory demands) or dismissed as "just metaphor."

### Mathematical Commitment Level

1. Is this paper intentionally **pre-formal**, or does it claim the existence of a precise mathematical structure?
2. Are events objects, morphisms, generators, or something prior to that distinction?
3. Is time a partial order, a total order, or merely a constraint on composition?

### Replay as Structure

4. Is replay a **path-independence condition**, a **flatness constraint**, or a **reconstructibility axiom**?
5. Are replay failures analogous to curvature, torsion, or obstruction — or is that analogy intentionally loose?
6. Is there a notion of holonomy you want reviewers to infer, or explicitly avoid?

### Equivalence & Quotients

% ==================================================
\section
*{Axiomatic Preamble: Semantic Structure, Replay, and Abstraction}
% ==================================================
This work proceeds from a small number of structural commitments concerning memory, semantics, and abstraction. These commitments are not intended as metaphysical claims about the ultimate constituents of reality, nor as prescriptions for any particular implementation or architecture. Rather, they are presented as axiomatic constraints: conditions that any system capable of abstraction, explanation, or stable meaning must satisfy, regardless of substrate or realization.

The role of this preamble is to fix the semantic gauge shared across all subsequent developments. Later sections may emphasize different consequences of these axioms depending on disciplinary context, but the axioms themselves remain invariant.
\subsection
*{Axiom 1: Event-Structured Semantics}

We assume that semantic structure is grounded in discrete commitments that may be ordered, replayed, and inspected. These commitments are referred to throughout as
\emph
{events}. An event is not assumed to be a physical occurrence, a psychological act, or a computational instruction, though it may coincide with any of these in particular realizations. Instead, an event is defined functionally as a minimal unit of semantic commitment: something that, once introduced, constrains what may follow.

Events are individuated by their role in a construction history rather than by intrinsic content. Two systems that differ only in their observational state but share the same event history are semantically indistinguishable under this framework. Conversely, systems that share observational outputs but differ in construction history may differ semantically.

This axiom rejects purely state-based accounts of meaning. States may summarize or present the consequences of events, but they do not replace the explanatory role of construction history.
\subsection
*{Axiom 2: Replayability}

A system admits semantic interpretation only insofar as its construction history is replayable. Replayability means that there exists a well-defined procedure—conceptual or operational—by which the semantic consequences of a sequence of events may be reconstructed from those events alone, without appeal to hidden state or external context.

Replayability is not assumed to be metaphysically primitive, nor is it required to be implemented at the level of physical determinism or bitwise reproducibility. The requirement is semantic: replay must reconstruct meaning, not necessarily microstate. Noise, approximation, and stochasticity are permitted so long as they do not alter semantic identity.

Replayability is treated here as an axiom rather than as an engineering convenience. Without replay, notions such as explanation, counterfactual reasoning, abstraction, and revision lose their coherence. A system that cannot, even in principle, replay its own construction history cannot distinguish between essential and accidental structure.
\subsection
*{Axiom 3: Separation of Cause and View}

Authoritative semantic structure is distinguished from all derived presentations, observations, or interpretations. Events constitute causes; views are consequences.

Derived artifacts—including visualizations, layouts, summaries, predictions, and speculative interpretations—are permitted and encouraged, but they are explicitly non-causal. They do not introduce new semantic commitments and may be discarded, reordered, or recomputed without altering the underlying meaning of the system.

This separation ensures that semantic structure is not contaminated by representational convenience. It also permits multiple, incompatible views to coexist over a single semantic history without contradiction.
\subsection
*{Axiom 4: Semantic Equivalence and Abstraction}

Abstraction is defined as the stabilization of semantic equivalence classes over replayable histories. Two constructions are semantically equivalent if, under admissible replay and perturbation, they yield the same semantic consequences. Abstraction occurs when multiple distinct constructions are treated as representatives of a single semantic effect.

This notion of abstraction is explicitly distinguished from compression. Compression reduces description length or resource usage; abstraction reduces the number of semantic representatives required to account for observed consequences. Compression may assist abstraction, but it does not entail it. In particular, compression alone cannot guarantee invariance under counterfactual variation.

Semantic equivalence is not imposed arbitrarily, nor is it merely discovered. It emerges when equivalence relations prove stable under replay. Abstraction is therefore neither purely subjective nor purely objective; it is constrained by structure.
\subsection
*{Axiom 5: Stability Under Perturbation}

Semantic structure is meaningful only to the extent that it is stable under admissible perturbations of construction history. A perturbation is any modification of event order, representation, or intermediate detail that preserves the conditions of replayability.

Stability admits degrees. Below a threshold of stability, behavior may generalize without abstraction; above it, semantic equivalence becomes robust. Abstraction is said to exist only when semantic identity survives perturbation across a nontrivial neighborhood of histories.

This axiom introduces a notion of semantic depth that is independent of behavioral performance or surface generalization.
\subsection
*{Axiom 6: Revision Without Erasure}

Abstraction is revisable but not freely reversible. Once semantic equivalences are stabilized, they may be refined, subdivided, or reorganized by the introduction of additional events, but they cannot be undone without cost. Revision requires new semantic commitments rather than rollback.

This asymmetry mirrors phenomena observed in learning, development, scientific theory change, and historical record-keeping. It also distinguishes semantic revision from mere error correction.
\subsection
*{Axiom 7: Substrate Neutrality}

The axioms above make no assumptions about physical substrate, representational medium, or implementation architecture. They apply equally to biological systems, artificial systems, social institutions, and formal structures, provided those systems admit event-structured memory and replayable construction histories.

Substrate neutrality does not imply implementation indifference. Different substrates may satisfy these axioms with different efficiencies, constraints, or failure modes. The axioms specify what must be true for abstraction to be possible, not how it must be realized.
\subsection
*{Scope and Non-Claims}

This framework does not attempt to define intelligence exhaustively, nor does it assert necessary or sufficient conditions for consciousness. It does not reduce meaning to entropy, representation, or behavior, though it is compatible with constraints arising from each. Where connections to thermodynamics, information theory, operating systems, or mathematical invariants are drawn, they are presented as structural correspondences rather than reductions.

What is claimed is narrower and stronger: any system capable of abstraction, explanation, or stable meaning must satisfy the axioms above. The remainder of each paper explores the consequences of this claim in a specific disciplinary context.

Article I — Minds and Machines

(Philosophical / Ontological Foundations)

Core Question Addressed

> What must abstraction be, in principle, for intelligence to be possible at all?



Main Arguments (Do NOT repeat verbatim elsewhere)

1. Abstraction is not representational

Abstraction cannot be grounded in symbols, representations, or states.

Any state-based or representational account presupposes abstraction rather than explaining it.

Therefore, abstraction must be grounded in process history, not stored content.



2. Events are ontologically prior to states

States are derivative summaries of event histories.

Semantic identity is determined by construction history, not instantaneous configuration.

This rejects state-based metaphysics without requiring eliminativism.



3. Replay is a necessary condition for abstraction

Without replay, equivalence across histories is undefinable.

Abstraction requires counterfactual comparison of "how this came to be."

Deterministic replay is not an implementation detail but a minimal explanatory posit.



4. Compression ≠ abstraction (in principle)

Compression reduces description length.

Abstraction reduces semantic degrees of freedom.

These are logically independent operations; conflating them causes category errors.



5. Semantic equivalence is historical

Two structures are abstractly identical iff their replayed consequences coincide.

This grounds abstraction in necessity, not convenience or efficiency.




Unique contribution of Article I
👉 Establishes what abstraction must be, regardless of brains, machines, or data.

Never repeat elsewhere:

Heavy metaphysical framing

Ontological necessity claims

Rejection of representationalism as such

Article II — Artificial Intelligence (Elsevier)

(Formal / Computational Distinctness)

Core Question Addressed

> What does abstraction give us that compression, generalization, or world models do not?



Main Arguments (Distinct from Article I)

1. Compression-only systems are provably insufficient

Systems that discard construction traces cannot distinguish invariant structure from coincidence.

This explains brittleness under distribution shift and recombination.



2. Replay defines a new computational capability class

Replayable systems can evaluate semantic equivalence under perturbation.

This capability is orthogonal to model size, data scale, or optimization power.



3. Equivalence is intensional, not extensional

Two components are equivalent if they yield identical effects under replay, not if outputs match on a dataset.

This separates abstraction from supervised generalization.



4. Representative collapse is semantic, not architectural

Abstraction occurs when multiple constructions map to one semantic representative.

This is not clustering, pruning, or compression, though it may co-occur with them.



5. Why LLMs fail without replay

Training history is erased into parameters.

No access to construction alternatives ⇒ no principled abstraction.

Language is already compressed; LLMs compress a compressed signal.



6. Formal falsifiability

A counterexample would be a non-replayable system that robustly preserves semantic invariants under intervention.

This sharpens the theory's empirical bite.




Unique contribution of Article II
👉 Shows abstraction is a missing computational primitive, not an optimization artifact.

Never repeat elsewhere:

Detailed AI failure analysis

Formal capability-class distinctions

Explicit LLM critique

Article III — Cognitive Science

(Developmental / Comparative / Functional Cognition)

Core Question Addressed

> How does abstraction arise in minds before language, symbols, or explicit reasoning?



Main Arguments (Distinct from I & II)

1. Abstraction is pre-linguistic

Infants and animals exhibit abstraction without symbols.

Therefore abstraction cannot depend on language or explicit representation.



2. Replayable memory is the cognitive substrate

Hippocampal replay, consolidation, and reactivation enable abstraction.

Memory is reconstructive, not archival.



3. Semantic equivalence precedes concepts

Abstraction stabilizes equivalence across experiences before categorization.

Concepts are downstream products, not foundations.



4. Compression explains prediction, not abstraction

Statistical learning supports prediction.

Abstraction requires preserving meaning under transformation, not just accuracy.



5. Developmental trajectory explained

Early overgeneralization → refinement via replay and feedback.

Abstraction sharpens as replay depth and consolidation increase.



6. Why animals outperform LLMs at abstraction

Animals replay embodied interaction histories.

LLMs lack access to construction-level memory.




Unique contribution of Article III
👉 Grounds abstraction in memory dynamics, not symbols or reasoning.

Never repeat elsewhere:

Infant/animal emphasis

Cognitive development framing

Neuroscience plausibility arguments

\begin
{abstract}
Information-theoretic accounts of intelligence and cognition have historically centered on compression, prediction, and entropy minimization. While these notions successfully explain statistical efficiency and generalization, they do not by themselves account for semantic stability: the persistence of meaning under perturbation, recombination, and historical variation. This paper develops a structural account of abstraction as a constraint-sensitive process operating under entropy, rather than as an entropy-reducing operation itself. By distinguishing compression from semantic equivalence, and entropy from invariance, we show that abstraction corresponds to a phase transition in the organization of replayable histories. Entropy constrains abstraction, but does not define it. The resulting framework clarifies why compression-heavy systems may remain semantically brittle, and why abstraction exhibits threshold behavior in complex adaptive systems.
\end
{abstract}
% ==================================================
\section
{Introduction}
% ==================================================
Information theory has provided some of the most powerful tools for understanding learning, inference, and adaptation. From Shannon entropy to rate-distortion theory, compression has been treated as both an explanatory and normative principle: intelligent systems, it is often argued, are those that discover compact representations of the regularities in their environment
\cite
{shannon1948,coverthomas}.

Yet compression alone does not explain why certain structures remain meaningful across transformations, interventions, and recombinations, while others collapse under minimal perturbation. Systems may achieve extraordinary compression while remaining semantically fragile, unable to preserve invariant structure when faced with novel contexts. This observation motivates a distinction that has remained underdeveloped in information-theoretic accounts: the distinction between reducing description length and stabilizing semantic equivalence.

This paper advances a structural account of abstraction that treats entropy as a constraint rather than as a defining principle. Abstraction is understood as the stabilization of equivalence classes over replayable construction histories, subject to entropic limits. Compression may facilitate this process, but abstraction itself corresponds to a different organizational regime—one characterized by robustness, invariance, and historical reconstructibility.

The central claim is not that entropy is irrelevant to abstraction, but that entropy operates orthogonally: it limits what can be stabilized, how deeply, and at what cost. Semantic stability emerges when systems organize their histories in ways that survive entropic perturbation, yielding a qualitative transition in behavior.
% ==================================================
\section
{Entropy as Constraint, Not Essence}
% ==================================================
Entropy enters cognitive and computational theories through multiple channels: uncertainty reduction, coding efficiency, thermodynamic cost, and irreversibility. These notions are often conflated, leading to ambiguity about what entropy is supposed to explain. In the present framework, entropy is treated neither as a semantic primitive nor as the substance of meaning. Instead, it functions as a boundary condition on what kinds of semantic structures can be maintained.

Shannon entropy quantifies uncertainty over symbol distributions, not over semantic effects. A representation may be maximally compressed in the Shannon sense while still encoding no stable semantic structure beyond the dataset on which it was trained. Conversely, a system may tolerate relatively high entropy while preserving invariant semantic relations across contexts.

Thermodynamic entropy further constrains abstraction by imposing costs on memory, replay, and revision. Replayable histories require energy to maintain, reconstruct, and perturb. These costs place upper bounds on the depth and breadth of abstraction, but they do not determine its form. Semantic equivalence is not identical to energetic efficiency, even though the two are coupled.
\begin
{proposition}
Entropy constrains the feasibility of abstraction without determining its semantic content.
\end
{proposition}

This distinction parallels that between syntax and semantics: entropy governs the space of possible encodings, while abstraction governs which equivalence relations remain stable within that space.
% ==================================================
\section
{Compression and Semantic Degrees of Freedom}
% ==================================================
Compression reduces the number of degrees of freedom required to encode data. Abstraction reduces the number of semantic representatives required to explain effects. These operations are related but non-identical.

A compressed representation may discard information that is irrelevant for prediction on a fixed distribution, yet that same information may be crucial for preserving semantic identity under intervention. Compression optimizes for average-case efficiency; abstraction optimizes for invariance across histories.

Consider two constructions that yield identical outputs on all observed inputs but differ in how those outputs were generated. Compression collapses them; abstraction distinguishes them until their equivalence is stabilized under replay. This distinction explains why compressed systems may generalize well yet fail catastrophically under recombination or counterfactual manipulation.
\begin
{definition}
The semantic degrees of freedom of a system are the number of distinct construction histories that must be preserved to maintain invariant effects under replay.
\end
{definition}

Abstraction reduces semantic degrees of freedom; compression reduces descriptive degrees of freedom. The former requires the latter only insofar as entropy budgets permit.
% ==================================================
\section
{Replay, Entropy, and Irreversibility}
% ==================================================
Replayability introduces an asymmetry into system dynamics. Construction histories accumulate irreversibly, even when abstractions are later formed. This asymmetry mirrors thermodynamic irreversibility, but the correspondence is structural rather than literal.

Replay allows systems to distinguish essential from accidental structure by comparing alternative histories. Each such comparison incurs entropic cost: storing alternatives, reconstructing trajectories, and evaluating perturbations require resources. As a result, abstraction cannot proceed indefinitely. It must operate within an entropy budget that limits how many distinctions can be tested and stabilized.
\begin
{theorem}
There exists an upper bound on abstraction depth determined by the entropy budget available for replay.
\end
{theorem}

This bound explains why abstraction often exhibits punctuated dynamics rather than smooth accumulation. Systems accrue construction histories until replay becomes costly, at which point abstraction collapses multiple histories into a stable equivalence. The process then repeats at a higher level.
% ==================================================
\section
{Phase Transitions in Semantic Organization}
% ==================================================
The transition from compression-dominated organization to abstraction-dominated organization exhibits features analogous to phase transitions in complex systems. Below a critical threshold of replay capacity, systems behave as pattern extractors: they compress, predict, and interpolate. Above that threshold, they reorganize around invariant semantic effects.

This transition is not merely quantitative. It involves a qualitative change in what counts as structure. Prior to abstraction, structure is statistical; afterward, it is historical and counterfactual.
\begin
{definition}
A semantic phase transition occurs when invariant equivalence classes emerge that are stable under admissible perturbations of construction history.
\end
{definition}

Such transitions depend on control parameters including memory depth, replay fidelity, and entropy export capacity. When these parameters cross critical values, semantic basins form: regions of construction space that map to the same semantic effect.

These basins may deepen, merge, or split as abstraction evolves, but their existence marks a categorical shift in system behavior.
% ==================================================
\section
{Stability, Robustness, and Entropic Noise}
% ==================================================
Semantic stability is not absolute. It is always defined relative to a class of perturbations and a noise model. Entropy introduces variability at every level: stochasticity in construction, noise in replay, and drift in representations.

Abstraction succeeds when semantic equivalence remains invariant across this variability. Robust abstraction therefore requires not minimal entropy, but controlled entropy: enough variability to test invariance, but not so much that replay collapses.

This perspective clarifies why systems optimized purely for entropy minimization may fail to abstract. Excessive regularization suppresses the very variability needed to identify invariant structure.
\begin
{proposition}
There exists an optimal entropy regime for abstraction, lying strictly between maximal order and maximal disorder.
\end
{proposition}

This result aligns with observations from complex adaptive systems, where maximal adaptability occurs near criticality rather than at extremes.
% ==================================================
\section
{Relation to Information-Theoretic Frameworks}
% ==================================================
The framework developed here is compatible with, but not reducible to, established information-theoretic approaches. Minimum description length captures trade-offs between model complexity and fit, but does not address semantic invariance under replay. Information bottleneck methods formalize relevant information compression, but presuppose a task-defined notion of relevance.

Free-energy-based accounts emphasize prediction error minimization, yet prediction accuracy does not entail abstraction. Semantic stability requires preserving equivalence across unobserved interventions, not merely minimizing surprise.

These frameworks can be understood as operating within a single semantic basin. Abstraction governs the formation and stabilization of those basins themselves.
% ==================================================
\section
{Implications for Scientific Explanation}
% ==================================================
Scientific theories exemplify abstraction beyond compression. A successful theory does not merely compress observations; it identifies invariant structures that remain stable across experimental interventions, measurement regimes, and historical reinterpretations.

From this perspective, scientific progress involves the stabilization of increasingly abstract equivalence classes over empirical histories. Entropy constrains how finely these classes can be resolved, but does not define their content.

This view explains both the power and the fragility of scientific abstraction: theories persist when their invariances survive perturbation, and collapse when new evidence reveals instability in their equivalence classes.
% ==================================================
\section
{Conclusion}
% ==================================================
Entropy constrains abstraction without defining it. Compression facilitates abstraction without guaranteeing it. Semantic stability emerges when replayable histories are organized into invariant equivalence classes that survive entropic perturbation.

By distinguishing entropy from semantics, and compression from abstraction, this paper clarifies why intelligent organization exhibits threshold behavior and why purely compression-driven systems remain brittle. Abstraction is not the elimination of information, but the stabilization of meaning under constraint.
\end

\title
{Semantic Consistency as a Systems Invariant:
\\
Replay, Abstraction, and Event-Sourced Computation}
\author

\begin

\maketitle
\begin
{abstract}
Operating systems, programming languages, and distributed systems traditionally ground correctness in state-based invariants, observational equivalence, or behavioral specifications. This paper develops an alternative foundation in which semantic consistency is defined over replayable event histories rather than instantaneous system states. Abstraction is treated as the stabilization of equivalence classes over construction histories, enforced by deterministic replay. We argue that many familiar system pathologies—state divergence, irreproducibility, brittle abstractions—arise from conflating correctness with state agreement rather than semantic invariance. By treating replay as an axiomatic constraint and abstraction as a semantic operation, we derive a systems model that cleanly separates authority from observation, causality from view, and construction from representation.
\end
{abstract}
% ==================================================
\section
{Introduction}
% ==================================================
Systems theory has long struggled with the tension between expressivity and correctness. As systems grow distributed, concurrent, and interactive, maintaining global invariants becomes increasingly difficult. Traditional approaches attempt to control this complexity through synchronization, consistency models, or increasingly sophisticated type systems. Yet many failures persist, not because systems compute incorrect values, but because their abstractions drift, fracture, or collapse under evolution.

This paper argues that the root cause lies in a category error: treating semantic correctness as a property of instantaneous system state rather than of construction history. State-based reasoning obscures causality, erases alternatives, and prevents principled abstraction. In contrast, event-sourced systems with deterministic replay make construction explicit and allow abstraction to be defined as an operation over histories.

The contribution here is not a new consistency model or synchronization protocol, but a reframing of what consistency means. Semantic consistency is defined as invariance under replay, not agreement on state. This shift enables abstractions that remain stable under refactoring, extension, and recomposition—properties increasingly essential in long-lived systems.
% ==================================================
\section
{State, Events, and Authority}
% ==================================================
State-based systems treat the current configuration of memory as authoritative. Events are ephemeral; once applied, they vanish into state. This architecture obscures causality and makes it impossible to reconstruct why a system is in its present configuration without auxiliary logging.

Event-sourced systems invert this relationship. Events are authoritative; state is derived. However, most practical event-sourced architectures treat replay as a convenience for recovery or auditing, not as a semantic constraint. As a result, abstraction remains informal and brittle.
\begin
{axiom}
The authoritative substrate of a semantic system is an append-only, totally ordered event history.
\end
{axiom}

This axiom enforces a strict separation between causation and observation. State becomes a cache, not a source of truth.
\begin
{proposition}
Any system whose authoritative semantics are state-based cannot, in general, support revisable abstraction without external bookkeeping.
\end
{proposition}

The proof follows from the fact that abstraction requires comparison across alternative histories, which state erasure precludes.
% ==================================================
\section
{Replay as a Semantic Constraint}
% ==================================================
Replayability is often framed as a debugging or recovery feature. Here it is treated as a semantic invariant: every abstract equivalence must be justifiable by replaying construction histories.

Deterministic replay ensures that the same event prefix always yields the same derived state. More importantly, it ensures that semantic equivalence can be tested by perturbing, reordering (where permitted), or partially replaying histories.
\begin
{definition}
A system is replay-consistent if all semantic claims made by the system are invariant under deterministic replay of its authoritative event history.
\end
{definition}

Replay-consistency is stronger than observational equivalence and orthogonal to linearizability or serializability. Two systems may be observationally indistinguishable while differing semantically if their construction histories diverge.
% ==================================================
\section
{Abstraction as Representative Collapse}
% ==================================================
Abstraction in systems is typically implemented implicitly: APIs hide details, types erase structure, modules encapsulate behavior. These mechanisms presume abstraction but do not define it.

In a replay-grounded system, abstraction is explicit. Multiple construction histories may be identified as semantically equivalent if they yield identical effects under all admissible replays. This identification induces a representative collapse: one semantic representative stands in for many histories.
\begin
{definition}
A semantic representative is an equivalence class of event histories invariant under replay.
\end
{definition}

Representative collapse is neither compression nor optimization. It is a semantic act that reduces degrees of freedom in future reasoning while preserving invariance.
\begin
{proposition}
Representative collapse is irreversible without access to the original construction histories.
\end
{proposition}

This irreversibility explains why abstraction carries cost and why premature abstraction leads to brittleness.
% ==================================================
\section
{Semantic Consistency vs. Correctness}
% ==================================================
Correctness traditionally refers to adherence to a specification. Semantic consistency refers to stability of meaning across system evolution.

A system may be correct at every step yet semantically inconsistent over time if abstractions drift. Conversely, a system may temporarily violate a specification while preserving semantic consistency.
\begin
{definition}
A system is semantically consistent if all abstractions remain invariant under admissible replay-preserving transformations.
\end
{definition}

This definition decouples semantic reasoning from low-level correctness and aligns better with long-lived, evolving systems.
% ==================================================
\section
{Concurrency, Distribution, and Total Order}
% ==================================================
Distributed systems often reject total order as impractical or unnecessary. However, rejecting total order at the semantic level conflates performance optimization with meaning.

This framework requires total order only at the level of authoritative events. Concurrency may exist in execution, but semantics are grounded in a single, replayable history.
\begin
{proposition}
Total semantic order is compatible with concurrent execution and does not imply synchronous operation.
\end
{proposition}

The total order functions as a semantic spine, not a scheduling constraint. This resolves many ambiguities in distributed abstraction.
% ==================================================
\section
{Programming Languages Implications}
% ==================================================
Programming languages traditionally enforce abstraction through types, modules, and effects. These mechanisms can be reinterpreted as constraints on admissible event histories rather than as properties of state.

Types restrict which constructions are legal; effects constrain which events may occur; modules delimit replay scope. None of these mechanisms require representational semantics when grounded in event history.

This perspective suggests a reorientation of PL theory: from type soundness as state safety to type soundness as replay invariance.
% ==================================================
\section
{Implementation Notes}
% ==================================================
A minimal system implementing these principles requires only:
(1) an append-only event log,
(2) a deterministic replay engine,
(3) a mechanism for declaring and enforcing semantic equivalence.

Performance optimizations—snapshots, caching, parallel replay—are derived concerns. They must preserve replay-consistency but are otherwise unconstrained.
% ==================================================
\section
{Limitations and Scope}
% ==================================================
This paper does not claim that replay-grounded semantics are sufficient for all systems. Real-time control, probabilistic systems, and hardware-level nondeterminism pose challenges.

However, these challenges arise at the boundary between semantics and physics, not from the framework itself. Where nondeterminism exists, it must be reified as events to preserve semantic reasoning.
% ==================================================
\section
{Conclusion}
% ==================================================
By grounding semantics in replayable event histories, systems can support abstraction as a first-class operation rather than an emergent accident. Semantic consistency emerges as an invariant distinct from correctness, performance, or availability.

This reframing clarifies longstanding tensions in OS design, PL theory, and distributed systems, and provides a foundation for abstractions that survive evolution rather than collapsing under it.
\end

Replay, Invariance, and the Geometry of Semantic Structure

\title
{Replay and Invariance:
\\
Toward a Structural Geometry of Semantic Systems}
\author

\maketitle
\begin
{abstract}
Modern physics and mathematics are unified not by ontology but by invariance. Physical laws, geometric structures, and mathematical objects are defined by what remains unchanged under admissible transformations. This paper develops a parallel structural framework for semantic systems, grounded in the notion of replayable construction histories. We treat replay as an axiomatic constraint analogous to path-independence or flatness conditions in geometry, and abstraction as the formation of equivalence classes invariant under replay. Without appealing to representational semantics or physical interpretation, we show that semantic stability admits a geometric description in terms of quotient structures, invariants, and structural phase transitions. The resulting framework positions meaning as an emergent invariant of construction, aligning semantic systems with the structural core of modern mathematics and physics.
\end
{abstract}
% ==================================================
\section
{Introduction}
% ==================================================
Across physics and mathematics, explanatory power arises not from enumerating objects but from identifying invariants. From classical mechanics to gauge theory, from topology to algebra, what distinguishes structure from accident is invariance under transformation. Meaningful structure persists; noise does not.

Semantic systems—whether scientific theories, mathematical formalisms, or computational constructions—exhibit a similar distinction. Some differences matter; others do not. Yet unlike physical systems, semantics has traditionally been treated as interpretive or representational rather than structural.

This paper advances a different position: semantic structure can be treated geometrically, as an invariant arising from replayable construction histories. Replay is not a metaphor for time reversal or simulation; it is an axiomatic constraint ensuring that meaning is defined by necessity rather than convenience. Abstraction, on this view, is not compression or symbol manipulation, but the formation of equivalence classes invariant under replay.

The goal is not to reduce semantics to physics, but to show that both obey the same structural logic.
% ==================================================
\section
{Structural Invariance as a Unifying Principle}
% ==================================================
In mathematics, an object is defined by its invariants under a group of transformations. In physics, laws are constraints invariant under changes of frame, gauge, or coordinates. In both cases, structure is identified with stability under admissible transformations.
\begin
{definition}
An invariant is a property preserved under a specified class of transformations.
\end
{definition}

The choice of admissible transformations is not arbitrary; it encodes the theory's commitments. Euclidean geometry permits rigid motions; gauge theories permit local phase shifts; topology permits continuous deformation.

Semantic systems likewise admit transformations: refactorings, re-derivations, alternative constructions. The question is which of these transformations preserve meaning.
% ==================================================
\section
{Replay as an Axiomatic Constraint}
% ==================================================
We introduce replay not as a dynamical process but as a structural axiom.
\begin
{axiom}
All admissible semantic transformations must preserve replayability of construction histories.
\end
{axiom}

Replayability asserts that a construction can be re-instantiated from its generative history without ambiguity. This axiom plays a role analogous to integrability conditions in differential geometry or path-independence in conservative systems.
\begin
{proposition}
Replayability enforces a notion of semantic flatness: equivalent constructions yield identical consequences under all admissible replays.
\end
{proposition}

This flatness does not imply triviality. Just as flat connections may admit rich global structure, replayable systems may support deep abstraction.
% ==================================================
\section
{Equivalence Classes and Quotient Structure}
% ==================================================
Abstraction arises when distinct constructions are identified as semantically equivalent. Structurally, this corresponds to forming a quotient space.
\begin
{definition}
A semantic equivalence class is a set of construction histories whose replayed consequences are identical under all admissible perturbations.
\end
{definition}

The space of constructions is partitioned into equivalence classes. The abstract object is not any representative construction, but the class itself.
\begin
{proposition}
Semantic quotienting reduces degrees of freedom while preserving invariant structure.
\end
{proposition}

This mirrors familiar constructions in mathematics, where quotienting by symmetry removes redundancy while revealing essential form.
\begin
{corollary}
Abstraction is neither lossless nor arbitrary: it is constrained by the requirement of replay invariance.
\end
{corollary}
% ==================================================
\section
{Geometry of Semantic Space}
% ==================================================
Although semantic spaces need not be manifolds, they admit geometric intuition. Distance may be defined operationally as divergence under replay; curvature as sensitivity to perturbation; stability as resistance to equivalence class fragmentation.
\begin
{definition}
Semantic distance between two constructions is the minimal perturbation under which their replayed consequences diverge.
\end
{definition}

Regions of low curvature correspond to robust abstractions; regions of high curvature indicate unstable or context-dependent meaning.

This geometry is not imposed but induced by replay constraints.
% ==================================================
\section
{Phase Transitions and Structural Change}
% ==================================================
In many systems, abstraction appears suddenly rather than gradually. Structurally, this corresponds to a phase transition in equivalence structure.
\begin
{proposition}
A semantic phase transition occurs when the topology of equivalence classes changes discontinuously under continuous variation of construction parameters.
\end
{proposition}

Examples include the emergence of conserved quantities, the stabilization of mathematical formalisms, or the crystallization of scientific theories.

Such transitions are structural, not psychological or sociological.
% ==================================================
\section
{Relation to Physical Theories}
% ==================================================
The framework developed here is deliberately interpretation-agnostic. Nevertheless, it resonates with several physical notions.

Replay resembles reversibility constraints but does not require time symmetry. Semantic flatness parallels gauge invariance without invoking fields. Quotienting echoes symmetry reduction without presupposing physical symmetry groups.
\begin
{proposition}
Semantic invariance is orthogonal to physical ontology: it constrains structure, not substance.
\end
{proposition}

This allows semantic systems to be compared structurally to physical systems without reductionism.
% ==================================================
\section
{Mathematics as Stabilized Replay}
% ==================================================
Mathematical objects may be viewed as maximally stable semantic equivalence classes. Proofs are constructions; theorems are invariants under replay.
\begin
{proposition}
A mathematical object is semantically defined by the invariance of its consequences under replay of admissible derivations.
\end
{proposition}

This explains why mathematical meaning is robust to notation, presentation, and proof strategy.
% ==================================================
\section
{Limitations and Open Directions}
% ==================================================
This paper does not provide a full formalization of semantic geometry. Nor does it claim that all semantic systems admit smooth structure.

Rather, it establishes replay invariance as a minimal axiom from which geometric reasoning becomes possible.

Future work may explore categorical formulations, topological invariants, or connections to physical information constraints, but such developments are extensions, not prerequisites.
% ==================================================
\section
{Conclusion}
% ==================================================
By treating replay as an axiomatic constraint and abstraction as invariant quotienting, semantic systems can be understood within the same structural framework that underlies modern physics and mathematics. Meaning emerges not from representation or interpretation, but from stability under transformation.

This perspective does not reduce semantics to geometry or physics. It shows that all three share a deeper commitment: structure is what survives change.
\end
{document}

{definition}[Construction System]

{C} = (
\mathcal
{E},
\mathcal
{H},
\mathcal
{R})

$

$

$
\mathcal

{H}

\mathcal
{S}
$

$
\mathcal
{S}
$
.

{H}

This axiom excludes stochastic or underdetermined replay but does not constrain how histories are generated.

h'

$
\mathcal
{H}
$
.
\end

Replay closure ensures that semantic construction is well-founded.

We now formalize abstraction as a quotient induced by replay invariance.

_

_

{H}
$

\[

_
1
\sim

_
2,

\mathcal

_

\mathcal
{R}(h
_

\begin

\mathcal
{H}
$
.

$
\mathcal
{S}
$

\begin

\[
\mathcal

\mathcal
{H} /

This formalizes abstraction as degree-of-freedom reduction constrained by replay.
Appendix C — Flatness and Path Independence
latex
Copy code
\section
{Replay Flatness}

\begin
{definition}[Semantic Flatness]
A construction system is semantically flat if for any two histories
$
h
_
1, h
_
2
$
with identical terminal event multisets,
\[
\mathcal
{R}(h
_
1) =
\mathcal
{R}(h
_
2).
\]
\end
{definition}

{proposition}
Semantic flatness implies that semantic outcome depends only on equivalence class, not construction path.
\end
{proposition}
This mirrors flat connections in geometry, without presupposing smooth structure.

Violations of flatness correspond to semantic curvature: local construction order affects global outcome.

\mathcal
{H}

that modifies a history by a bounded change (event substitution, deletion, or insertion).

\begin
{definition}[Semantic Distance]
The semantic distance between histories
$
h
_

$
and
$

_

$
is
\[
d(h
_

_
2) =
\inf
\{
\|
\delta
\|
:
\mathcal
{R}(
\delta
(h
_

\neq
\mathcal
{R}(
\delta
(h
_
2))
\}
.
\]
\end
{definition}
This distance need not satisfy the triangle inequality globally; it defines a pseudometric on equivalence classes.

\begin
{definition}[Semantic Stability]
An equivalence class
$
[h]
\in
\mathcal
{Q}
$
is stable if
\[
\exists
\epsilon
> 0
\text
{ such that } d(h,h') >
\epsilon
\text
{ for all } h'
\notin
[h].
\]
\end
{definition}
Stable classes correspond to robust abstractions.

\section
{Scope Clarifications}
For avoidance of misinterpretation, we explicitly state:
No assumption of smooth manifolds
No reliance on category theory
No identification of semantic space with physical spacetime
No claim that replay is physically realizable in all systems
No reduction of meaning to entropy or information

\appendix
\section
{Replay Axiomatics}

We begin by formalizing replay as a structural constraint on construction systems. The aim is to isolate the minimal assumptions required to speak meaningfully about abstraction, equivalence, and invariance, without presupposing any particular computational, physical, or representational substrate.
\begin
{definition}[Construction System]
A construction system is a tuple
\[
\mathcal
{C} = (
\mathcal
{E},
\mathcal
{H},
\mathcal
{R})
\]
where
$
\mathcal
{E}
$
is a set of primitive events,
$
\mathcal
{H}
$
is the set of finite event histories (finite sequences over
$
\mathcal
{E}
$
), and
$
\mathcal
{R} :
\mathcal
{H}
\to
\mathcal
{S}
$
is a replay map assigning each history a semantic outcome in a space
$
\mathcal
{S}
$
.
\end
{definition}

The replay map need not be injective. Multiple distinct histories may correspond to the same semantic outcome.
\begin
{axiom}[Replay Determinacy]
For any history
$
h
\in
\mathcal
{H}
$
, the replay map
$
\mathcal
{R}(h)
$
is uniquely determined.
\end
{axiom}

This axiom excludes stochastic or underdetermined replay, but does not constrain how histories are generated, nor does it require that histories be physically reproducible.
\begin
{axiom}[Replay Closure]
If
$
h
\in
\mathcal
{H}
$
, then any prefix
$
h'
\preceq
h
$
is also an element of
$
\mathcal
{H}
$
.
\end
{axiom}

Replay closure ensures that semantic construction is well-founded and that intermediate stages of construction are themselves meaningful within the system.
\section
{Semantic Equivalence and Quotient Structure}

Abstraction is formalized as a quotient induced by replay invariance.
\begin
{definition}[Replay Equivalence]
Two histories
$
h
_
1, h
_
2
\in
\mathcal
{H}
$
are replay-equivalent, written
$
h
_
1
\sim
h
_
2
$
, if and only if
\[
\mathcal
{R}(h
_
1) =
\mathcal
{R}(h
_
2).
\]
\end
{definition}
\begin
{proposition}
Replay equivalence is an equivalence relation on
$
\mathcal
{H}
$
.
\end
{proposition}
\begin
{proof}
Reflexivity and symmetry follow immediately from equality. Transitivity follows from the transitivity of equality in the semantic space
$
\mathcal
{S}
$
.
\end
{proof}
\begin
{definition}[Semantic Quotient Space]
The semantic quotient space is defined as
\[
\mathcal
{Q} =
\mathcal
{H} /
\sim
,
\]
whose elements are equivalence classes of replay-equivalent histories.
\end
{definition}

The quotient is induced by necessity, not by optimization or representational convenience.
\begin
{proposition}
The canonical projection
$
\pi
:
\mathcal
{H}
\to
\mathcal
{Q}
$
is surjective but generally not injective.
\end
{proposition}

This non-injectivity captures the core notion of abstraction: many constructions collapse onto a single semantic representative.
\section
{Replay Flatness and Path Independence}

Replay induces a flatness condition analogous to path independence in geometric and algebraic settings.
\begin
{definition}[Semantic Flatness]
A construction system is semantically flat if, for any two histories
$
h
_
1, h
_
2
\in
\mathcal
{H}
$
that differ only by permutation of independent events, the replay outcomes coincide:
\[
\mathcal
{R}(h
_
1) =
\mathcal
{R}(h
_
2).
\]
\end
{definition}
\begin
{proposition}
Semantic flatness implies that semantic outcome depends only on equivalence class, not on construction path.
\end
{proposition}
\begin
{corollary}
Violations of semantic flatness correspond to semantic curvature, in which local construction order affects global outcome.
\end
{corollary}

No smoothness, differentiability, or manifold structure is assumed. The analogy to curvature is purely structural.
\section
{Semantic Distance and Stability}

To reason about robustness and invariance, we introduce a minimal notion of semantic distance.
\begin
{definition}[Perturbation]
A perturbation is a function
\[
\delta
:
\mathcal
{H}
\to
\mathcal
{H}
\]
that modifies a history by a bounded change, such as event substitution, deletion, or insertion.
\end
{definition}
\begin
{definition}[Semantic Distance]
The semantic distance between histories
$
h
_
1
$
and
$
h
_
2
$
is defined as
\[
d(h
_
1, h
_
2) =
\inf
\{
\|
\delta
\|
\mid
\mathcal
{R}(
\delta
(h
_
1))
\neq
\mathcal
{R}(
\delta
(h
_
2))
\}
.
\]
\end
{definition}

This function defines a pseudometric on equivalence classes; it need not satisfy the triangle inequality globally.
\begin
{definition}[Semantic Stability]
An equivalence class
$
[h]
\in
\mathcal
{Q}
$
is semantically stable if there exists
$
\epsilon
> 0
$
such that for all
$
h'
\notin
[h]
$
,
\[
d(h, h') >
\epsilon
.
\]
\end
{definition}

Stable equivalence classes correspond to abstractions that persist under bounded perturbations.
\section
{Structural Phase Transitions}

We now formalize abstraction emergence as a structural phase transition.
\begin
{definition}[Control Parameter]
A control parameter
$
\lambda
$
is a continuous parameter governing admissible perturbations or construction constraints within a family of construction systems
$
\mathcal
{C}
_
\lambda
$
.
\end
{definition}
\begin
{definition}[Semantic Phase Transition]
A semantic phase transition occurs at
$
\lambda
=
\lambda
_
c
$
if the topology or connectivity structure of the quotient space
$
\mathcal
{Q}
_
\lambda
$
changes discontinuously at
$
\lambda
_
c
$
.
\end
{definition}
\begin
{proposition}
Semantic phase transitions correspond to qualitative changes in equivalence-class stability, connectivity, or degeneracy.
\end
{proposition}

This definition captures abstraction emergence without appeal to thermodynamic entropy or statistical mechanics.
\section
{Mathematical Objects as Invariants}

Mathematical objects arise naturally as maximally stable semantic invariants.
\begin
{definition}[Admissible Derivation]
An admissible derivation is a history whose events correspond to rule-governed inferential steps within a formal system.
\end
{definition}
\begin
{proposition}
A mathematical object corresponds to a semantic equivalence class invariant under all admissible derivations.
\end
{proposition}
\begin
{corollary}
Mathematical meaning is independent of representational form, proof path, or syntactic encoding.
\end
{corollary}

This provides a structural account of mathematical objecthood without epistemic or psychological assumptions.
\section
{Scope and Non-Commitments}

To avoid misinterpretation, we make the following clarifications explicit.

No assumption is made that semantic spaces are smooth manifolds. No reliance is placed on category theory, though the framework is compatible with later categorical refinement. No identification is made between semantic space and physical spacetime. No claim is made that replay is physically realizable in all systems. No reduction of meaning to entropy or information is assumed.
\begin
{axiom}[Minimality]
Only structural commitments explicitly stated in this paper are assumed.
\end
{axiom}
